### Unguarded Deck Machinery and Dynamic Strike Hazards *(Exhibited in 8 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by crew members sustaining serious injuries — including lacerations, amputations, and crush injuries — during routine deck operations involving dynamic energy sources such as rotating capstans, tensioned lines, chain assemblies, and strong magnetic components. The unifying feature across these incidents is the absence of a defined safe system of work that controls either the energy source itself or the positioning of personnel relative to it. Tasks are routinely performed without formal risk assessment, without physical guarding or tool-assisted methods, and without procedural boundaries that enforce standby or abort criteria when conditions deteriorate. The recurrence of these incidents reflects a systemic normalisation of manual interaction with hazardous deck machinery in high-variability environments, where the consequences of foreseeable deviations — vessel movement, gear becoming fast, unexpected component behaviour — are not mitigated at the design or procedural level.

Latent hardware deficiencies, including the absence of capstan nip-point guards and the lack of tool-assisted methods for chain manipulation, directly expose crew to contact injury when vessel motion or component behaviour deviates from expectation. Human factors compound this exposure: instinctive postural corrections, multi-tasking under load, and misjudgement of dynamic line or chain behaviour are predictable responses to an unstable working environment that task design fails to account for. Environmental conditions — night operations, sea states at or above Category 4, and wind-driven vessel movement — systematically degrade situational awareness, reduce reaction time, and increase the unpredictability of machinery and line behaviour. Critically, these environmental factors are known at the time of task commencement yet consistently fail to trigger formal risk review or task modification, indicating that the procedural framework governing these operations does not adequately integrate environmental risk thresholds into go/no-go decision-making.

- **Occurrence 491d654a-60dd-ef11-8eea-002248c71edd**: A tug crew member was letting go of headlines, when the vessel moved and the weight came back on suddenly. They then got their fingers trapped on their right hand and whilst trying to release the line

### Hydraulic System Failures Causing Undetected Marine Oil Pollution *(Exhibited in 10 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by the failure of hydraulic system components — including hoses, seals, pipe-to-coupling connections, and valves — across a range of vessel types and operational contexts, resulting in oil releases to the marine environment. A consistent pattern emerges in which mechanical degradation (through vibration fatigue, wear, or seal deterioration) goes undetected prior to failure, reflecting systemic weaknesses in planned maintenance regimes and pre-operational inspection protocols. Critically, in the majority of incidents, the volume of oil discharged could not be quantified, undermining both environmental impact assessment and regulatory compliance obligations under MARPOL. The recurrence of these incidents across cable-laying vessels, tugs, cruise ships, dredgers, and ro-pax ferries indicates that this is not an equipment-type-specific problem but rather a sector-wide vulnerability in how pressurised hydraulic systems on deck machinery are maintained, monitored, and responded to when failures occur.

Latent hardware deficiencies — including deferred maintenance, inadequate inspection frequencies, and the use of components operating beyond their effective service life — form the technical foundation of these incidents. These hardware vulnerabilities are compounded by human and procedural factors, most notably the absence of structured overnight or heavy weather deck monitoring, confirmation bias leading crews to attribute sheens to external sources rather than their own vessel, and the lack of established 'vessel-first' pollution response protocols. Environmental conditions, particularly night-time darkness and heavy weather sea states, repeatedly reduced crew detection capability and delayed intervention, allowing discharges to continue unobserved and rendering post-incident quantification impossible due to seawater dilution. Collectively, these factors reveal a latent organisational tolerance for reactive rather than proactive hydraulic system risk management, which must be addressed through both procedural reform and enhanced technical oversight.

**Representative Cases:**
- **Occurrence 24aee4ef-bd84-f011-b4cc-002248c611f0**: A cable laying vessel reported that hydraulic oil had leaked from their plough. The plough was recovered, the quantity of oil that had leaked was unknown at the time.
- **Occurrence 627973a4-8f6b-f011-b4cb-0022481a4e86**: A tug was operating in a harbour when the crew noticed there was a leak of hydraulic oil under pressure from the aft winch. The pumps were immediately shut down and the crew contained as much of the o
- **Occurrence 0108105d-b4cd-ef11-b8e8-000d3a86e2d0**: A cruise ship was on passage during heavy weather when a small pipe from hydraulic system on the mooring deck disconnected from the coupling, releasing an unknown amount of hydraulic oil over the moor

### Fire and Explosion from Uncontrolled Ignition Sources Onboard *(Exhibited in 9 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by fires and explosions arising from the accumulation of flammable vapours or unregulated ignition sources in spaces that fall outside routine crew monitoring or formal hazard control frameworks. Across the incidents examined, a consistent pattern emerges: flammable conditions develop undetected — whether through fuel system leaks aboard recreational and cargo vessels, or through unregulated guest behaviour on cruise ship balconies — and are subsequently ignited due to absent or inadequate procedural controls. The recurring failure to identify and mitigate ignition risk before it manifests reflects a systemic deficiency in pre-operational checks, hazardous area protocols, and the enforcement of safe work practices. Night-time conditions further compound detection failures, reducing the crew's capacity to identify physical warning signs such as fuel vapour, spills, or nascent fire.

Latent hardware failures — including fuel system integrity deficiencies, absent or non-functional vapour detection equipment, and thermally vulnerable glazing — create the preconditions for these events. Human factors are equally prominent: complacency following incomplete venting periods, use of non-intrinsically safe devices in hazardous atmospheres, and failure to conduct formal atmosphere testing or hazard assessments before re-entering compromised spaces all recur across incidents. Environmental conditions, particularly night-time darkness and open-deck wind exposure, amplify both ignition risk and the speed of fire development. Together, these factors reveal a latent systemic vulnerability in which procedural non-compliance and inadequate safety equipment intersect with poorly supervised or unmonitored spaces, creating conditions that make escalation to a serious casualty highly probable.

### Alcohol Impairment and Inadequate Oversight Causing Overboard Incidents *(Exhibited in 8 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by incidents in which alcohol intoxication — affecting skippers, crew members, and passengers alike — serves as the primary proximate cause of falls overboard, serious injury, and life-threatening situations. Across all representative incidents, intoxicated individuals demonstrate impaired balance, degraded judgement, and reduced capacity to self-preserve or respond to rescue, with outcomes ranging from hypothermia and compound fractures to a broken neck and near-drowning. The recurrence of these incidents is not incidental; it reflects a systemic failure of pre-departure and in-voyage fitness-for-duty checks, ineffective alcohol service controls, and — critically — the absence of meaningful enforcement action by harbour authorities and vessel operators even where patterns of alcohol-related behaviour were already documented. The incidents do not represent isolated lapses; they represent the predictable endpoint of known risks that were not acted upon.

While hardware deficiencies are minor contributors in this cluster, the dominant latent causes are human and procedural in nature. Environmental conditions — including night-time darkness, adverse sea states (in some cases more severe than officially recorded), and moderate wind — significantly amplified the consequences of alcohol-impaired decision-making, reducing the margin for error to near zero. The absence of personal flotation devices, safety watches, physical barriers at vessel peripheries, and passenger welfare monitoring protocols compounded individual vulnerability into collective systemic failure. Where environmental data discrepancies exist between logged and recorded sea states, this also raises concerns about the reliability of condition assessments used to sanction vessel operations, suggesting that procedural controls at the gateway to departure were either absent or ineffective across multiple vessel types and operational contexts.

### Bilge System Failure and Inadequate Crew Familiarisation *(Exhibited in 13 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by flooding emergencies in which vessels' primary dewatering systems fail at the critical moment they are needed, transforming manageable water ingress into life-safety emergencies requiring external rescue intervention. Across the representative incidents, a consistent pattern emerges: the bilge pump — whether rendered inoperable by mechanical failure, electrical power loss, or lack of maintenance — constitutes a single point of failure with no redundant backup. Compounding this, crew are frequently unfamiliar with vessel-specific systems, particularly in bareboat charter contexts, delaying identification of the ingress source and the isolation of contributing valves or fittings. The recurring outcome — vessels requiring SAR assistance or towing to safety — indicates that these incidents are not isolated equipment failures but systemic deficiencies in vessel readiness and crew preparedness.

Latent hardware deficiencies are the dominant enabling condition in this theme, manifesting as unmaintained bilge pumps, fractured pump handles, failed electrical systems, and the absence of independently powered or manual backup dewatering capacity. Human factors intersect critically with these hardware failures: inadequate pre-departure checks allow non-functional equipment to go undetected, while the absence of structured handover processes in charter arrangements leaves crew without the system knowledge necessary to respond effectively under pressure. Environmental conditions — particularly adverse sea states and night-time operations — consistently amplify the consequences by accelerating water ingress beyond manageable levels and reducing the crew's situational awareness and response effectiveness. Together, these latent conditions align to convert foreseeable, preventable equipment states into active flooding emergencies.

### Electrical Hardware Failures Escalating to Fire Through Maintenance Gaps *(Exhibited in 57 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by small but operationally significant fire events initiated by latent electrical hardware defects — including overheating transformers, degraded plugs, faulty pre-heaters, defective valve spindles, and failed alternators — across a diverse range of vessel types and operational contexts. In every case, the immediate ignition event was preceded by an undetected component-level fault that had been allowed to develop within a preventive maintenance regime insufficiently rigorous to identify degraded or substandard equipment before failure. The high proportion of procedural and hardware contributing factors confirms that these incidents are not random or unforeseeable; they represent a systemic pattern in which maintenance programmes are not effectively closing the gap between scheduled sign-off and the actual physical condition of electrical and mechanical components returned to service. The recurrence of this pattern across vessel classes suggests an industry-wide normalisation of risk around electrical maintenance standards.

Latent hardware vulnerabilities — components operating beyond their thermal rating, with degraded insulation, or in environmentally challenging enclosures — form the foundational failure mode in this cluster. These defects are inherently difficult to detect without component-level inspection protocols that go beyond visual checks or operational testing alone. Human factors, including reduced crew vigilance during night-time operations and the implicit trust placed in maintenance records over physical verification, further reduce the likelihood of pre-failure identification. Environmental conditions such as condensation in cold cabinets or elevated ambient temperatures in machinery spaces accelerate degradation while simultaneously reducing inspection frequency. Critically, the absence of overtemperature protection devices across multiple incidents indicates a systemic gap in engineering safeguards: where prevention fails, no secondary barrier exists to arrest escalation before smoke, alarm activation, or open fire. Effective mitigation requires both improved component-level inspection standards and the mandatory fitting of thermal protection on susceptible electrical circuits.

### Submerged Object Contact in Low-Visibility Conditions *(Exhibited in 34 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by vessel contact with uncharted, transient, or poorly marked submerged and surface objects during night-time or low-water operations, predominantly in coastal, port approach, and inshore environments. The incidents recur because routine and operational transits—including maintenance runs, passenger services, and SAR exercises—are conducted without tidal-state-adjusted passage plans or night-specific risk protocols. Operators frequently treat familiar routes as inherently low-risk, leading to a normalisation of conditions that in fact carry compounding hazards: reduced under-keel clearance at low water, repositioned or newly introduced debris, and the near-complete elimination of visual detection capability in darkness. The absence of systematic channel monitoring and hazard reporting infrastructure means that transient objects, waterlogged markers, and uncharted obstructions enter the navigable environment without generating any navigational warning, leaving vessels with no advance indication of the hazard in their path.

Latent hardware deficiencies are a consistent thread across this cluster, with no evidence of forward-looking sonar, echo sounder alert systems, or equivalent real-time underwater detection equipment being operational or effective at the point of contact. This reflects either an absence of such equipment on the vessel types involved, a failure to activate or monitor available systems, or a reliance on visual watchkeeping that is structurally inadequate under night conditions. Human factors compound these gaps: passage planning does not routinely incorporate tidal state as a dynamic risk variable, and watchkeepers appear to have insufficient procedural guidance for detecting low-profile or submerged hazards in poor visibility. Environmental conditions—predominantly darkness, combined in some cases with wind-driven surface disturbance obscuring anomalies—remove the last available layer of detection. The convergence of absent detection technology, inadequate tidal-aware planning, and degraded visual conditions creates a latent failure pathway that is triggered repeatedly across different vessel types and operational contexts.

### Electrical and Battery System Fires Aboard Marine Vessels *(Exhibited in 10 incidents)*

This theme encompasses a recurring pattern of fire and explosion events originating from electrical systems, battery banks, and charging equipment across a diverse range of vessel types — from commercial workboats and narrowboats to passenger cruise ships and cargo vessels. The common characteristic is the failure of an electrical or battery component, most frequently manifesting as thermal runaway, short circuit, overcharging, or gas accumulation, in an environment where early detection and suppression capabilities are absent or inadequate. A consistent and critical finding across these incidents is the reliance on third parties — adjacent vessel crews, marina staff, or passing members of the public — to identify fire outbreaks, rather than onboard automated detection systems or vigilant watchkeepers. This delayed detection systematically allows localised and potentially containable electrical faults to escalate into fully developed fires, explosions, and total vessel losses, with associated risk to life including responding emergency personnel.

The latent hardware vulnerabilities underlying these incidents include inadequate battery management systems (BMS), poorly designed or maintained charging infrastructure, insufficient ventilation in battery compartment spaces, and the inherent flammability of vessel construction materials such as timber. These hardware deficiencies are compounded by procedural failures: unattended overnight charging without safeguards, absence of routine inspection regimes for battery installations, lack of vessel-specific emergency response protocols for electric or hybrid propulsion systems, and inadequate crew training on the explosion risks posed by hydrogen off-gassing from batteries during charging or thermal events. Environmental factors, particularly night-time conditions, further reduce the probability of early visual detection and complicate emergency response coordination. Collectively, these latent conditions create a systemic vulnerability in which the convergence of a single hardware fault with inadequate detection, suppression, and procedural safeguards produces disproportionately severe outcomes. The increasing proliferation of electric and hybrid propulsion systems across the maritime sector makes resolution of these latent factors an urgent safety priority.

- **Occurrence 059a8aae-3a87-f011-b4cc-0022481adc5c**: A narrowboat with an electric motor experienced a fire. The vessel was alongside in a marina with no crew on board. The boat started smoking and the fire brigade attended. Once they had arrived the bo
- **Occurrence 3e821eed-35fa-ed11-8f6d-002248c87217**: A wooden pleasure yacht caught fire, the single crewmember on board abandoned to the dinghy and was then rescued by another pleasure sailing yacht and the vessel burned down to the bilges. The wreck s

Across these incidents, the common thread is not acute damage from a single catastrophic event, but rather the slow development of a critical structural deficiency that existing inspection and maintenance regimes failed to identify. The incidents recur because structural degradation in areas such as engine room shell plating, skeg welds, and underwater hull sections is inherently difficult to detect without targeted, methodical inspection programmes, and because operational and commercial pressures can allow survey intervals to become the ceiling rather than the floor of structural oversight.

### Undetected Flammable and Toxic Gas Accumulation Aboard Vessels *(Exhibited in 14 incidents)*

This theme encompasses incidents arising from the undetected accumulation of toxic or flammable gases — principally carbon monoxide (CO) and liquefied petroleum gas (LPG) — within enclosed or semi-enclosed spaces aboard vessels. The incidents share a consistent causal architecture: a latent hardware deficiency, whether a compromised exhaust or combustion system, a leaking LPG tank, or a defective fuel system, allows dangerous gases to migrate or accumulate in occupied or unattended spaces. This hazard is compounded in every case by the absence or failure of automated detection systems, meaning that no independent safeguard exists to interrupt the progression toward a casualty event. The pattern recurs across vessel types — from small pleasure craft to commercial utility and crew transfer vessels — indicating that the underlying safety management failures are systemic rather than isolated. The asymmetry between severity outcomes, ranging from minor injury to vessel loss and animal fatality, reflects the degree to which chance, occupancy, and proximity to an ignition source determine consequences rather than any meaningful variation in underlying risk control.

Latent hardware deficiencies are the initiating condition in each incident, but the absence of procedural safeguards consistently transforms a manageable technical failure into a serious casualty. Crews and owners demonstrated no established pre-operation checks — such as gas sniff tests, bilge ventilation procedures, or exhaust system inspections — that would have intercepted accumulation before exposure or ignition occurred. Human factors, including failure to recognise early CO poisoning symptoms, the diminished alertness associated with night-time operation, and the reduced procedural rigour characteristic of single-handed or leisure vessel operation, further eroded the opportunity for timely intervention. Environmental conditions, notably low wind speeds and enclosed berthing arrangements, reduced natural ventilation effectiveness and accelerated accumulation rates. Collectively, these incidents demonstrate a well-documented failure mode in which the safety system has no depth: when the primary hardware barrier fails, no procedural, detection-based, or human-recognition layer exists to prevent harm.

**Representative Cases:**
- **Occurrence 02b57967-783a-ee11-bdf4-002248c8796d**: A utility vessel had returned to port and was alongside. A crew member went to hospital as they had been feeling unwell whilst at sea. Following an assessment and blood tests, the crew member was foun
- **Occurrence d4923e8d-aea0-ed11-aad1-0022481b5a3b**: A sailing vessel with one person onboard was moored on a river, the casualty went to light a stove but the gas exploded, probably due to the tank leaking. The casualty suffered burns to the face and t
- **Occurrence 577a4670-fd61-ee11-8df0-002248c878ca**: Inland pleasure vessel sank after an explosion. It was alleged that a kettle had been left on the gas ring while the owner was fishing on the bank. No injuries reported.

### Undetected Hull Ingress Pathways and Inadequate Bilge Safeguards *(Exhibited in 22 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by flooding incidents arising from latent deficiencies in hull integrity systems — principally failed or deteriorating through-hull fittings, exhaust system components, and seacocks — that go undetected until water ingress has reached a critical level. A consistent pattern across incidents is the absence of any effective first line of defence: bilge pumps are undersized or non-functional, bilge alarms are absent or inoperable, and pre-departure or routine maintenance inspections have failed to identify the degradation of components that are known high-risk ingress pathways. Where vessels are left unattended at berth without watchkeeping arrangements or remote monitoring, the situation is further compounded, with chance observation by members of the public becoming the sole means of detection. The incidents span both underway and alongside contexts, indicating that the underlying procedural and equipment deficiencies are systemic rather than situational.

Latent hardware failure is the proximate cause in the majority of cases within this cluster, but the severity and frequency of these incidents is sustained by compounding human and environmental factors. Inadequate familiarity with a vessel's through-hull configuration, insufficient routine inspection regimes, and an absence of formalised pre-departure watertight integrity checks reflect systemic procedural shortcomings at the owner and operator level. Environmental conditions — particularly night operations and moderate sea states — reduce the crew's ability to detect ingress early, locate its source, or conduct effective damage control. The absence of operable VHF radio communications in several incidents further delayed distress alerting, demonstrating that multiple independent safeguards were either absent or had silently degraded prior to the emergency. Collectively, these incidents illustrate how the convergence of unmonitored hardware deterioration, procedural inattention, and environmental challenge transforms a preventable maintenance failure into a SAR-requiring emergency.

- **Occurrence acca5872-e015-f011-998a-6045bd0d3022**: A sailing yacht under engine started taking on water, as the bilges started to fill, the engine stopped and smoke was seen but no fire. The water ingress then stopped but both the bilges and engine ba
- **Occurrence e73eff6c-7952-f011-877b-002248c61a33**: A charter fishing vessel was alongside, it was reported by members of the public that the vessel was sinking. A shore worker attended the scene as the vessel was unoccupied. The vessel was secured wit
- **Occurrence 090e53bc-c513-f011-998a-6045bd0d3022**: A sailing yacht started taking on water, the pump was unable to cope with the ingress and the source of the ingress was unknown. A nearby vessel offered assistance until a search and rescue (SAR) vess

---

### Undetected Hull and Machinery Deficiencies Causing Flooding *(Exhibited in 40 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by flooding incidents arising from latent structural and mechanical deficiencies — including failed through-hull fittings, degraded hoses, deteriorated pump casings, and compromised hull integrity — that were not identified prior to the flooding event. The incidents span recreational vessels, commercial fishing vessels, and SAR platforms, indicating the issue is not confined to any single sector. A consistent pattern emerges whereby the underlying physical vulnerability had been developing over time through corrosion, material fatigue, rot, or wear, yet no pre-departure or routine inspection regime was in place or sufficiently rigorous to detect it. Once flooding commenced, inadequate onboard bilge pumping capacity, the absence of damage control materials, and — in several cases — the vessel being operated at night or the crew being asleep, allowed water ingress to progress to a critical or life-threatening stage before effective intervention was possible.

Latent hardware deficiencies represent the foundational cause across this cluster, but their consequences were consistently amplified by compounding human and environmental factors. Absent or inadequate maintenance and pre-departure seaworthiness checks reflect systemic failures in safety culture and regulatory compliance, particularly among recreational vessel operators. Human factors — including limited crew knowledge of damage control procedures, delayed detection due to night-time operations, and sole operators being asleep — removed opportunities for early intervention at each stage of the flooding sequence. Environmental conditions, including rough seas and confused swell, introduced additional mechanical stress and complicated emergency response. The recurrence of these incidents across vessel types and operational contexts demonstrates that without structured inspection regimes, minimum onboard damage control capability, and crew competency in emergency flooding response, latent hardware vulnerabilities will continue to manifest as serious or potentially fatal flooding events.

### Crew Injury from Falls During Night Operations *(Exhibited in 36 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by crew injuries resulting from falls, trips, and loss of balance occurring predominantly during night-time operations across a range of vessel types, from sailing yachts to offshore supply ships and replenishment tankers. The incidents share a consistent pattern: a crewmember is moving around or working on deck or below in reduced visibility conditions, often in adverse sea states, without adequate procedural controls governing safe movement or task execution. The consequences range from dislocated hips and broken limbs to head and neck trauma, and in several cases the injury itself generated secondary operational emergencies — most notably where a minimal crew complement was rendered unable to complete basic vessel handling after a single casualty. These incidents recur because the combination of night conditions, vessel motion, and structural or task-specific hazards is predictable yet insufficiently controlled at the procedural and organisational level.

The latent conditions underpinning this theme operate across all three principal factor domains. Environmentally, reduced night-time visibility degrades hazard perception, while moderate wind and sea states introduce vessel motion that directly destabilises crew footing and working posture. Hardware factors include fixed structural hazards such as canopy frames and open bilges positioned in proximity to crew movement and working areas, unguarded deck obstructions, and fittings that require excessive force to release — all of which amplify injury severity when a fall occurs. Human factors are consistently rooted in the absence of pre-task risk assessment, failure to adopt stable three-point contact postures or use available safety equipment such as jackstays and handholds, and inadequate situational awareness in low-light conditions. At the organisational level, the defining latent failure is the absence of robust procedural controls — including safe movement protocols for night passages, minimum safe manning risk assessments, and task-level hazard identification for routine maintenance — that would interrupt the chain of causation before injury occurs.

- **Occurrence dd787eec-a472-f011-b4cc-0022481ad514**: Sailing yacht was in passage when a crew member suffered a fall onboard which resulted in them ramming against the canopy with their head, also additionally a jarred neck injury was also sustained to 
- **Occurrence cadf622c-ed2b-f011-8c4e-002248c61a33**: A sailing vessel crewmember broke their leg after tripping on deck. The other crew member was not able to bring the vessel alongside on their own so they called for assistance. The marina staff helped
- **Occurrence 57ff29dd-f4ce-ed11-a7c6-002248c87217**: An crew member of a replenishment tanker was working in the engine room on a generator fuel housing using a spanner to remove the fitting. The fitting was tight and required the crewmember to exert so

### Solo Passage Failures: Redundancy, Competency, and Preparedness *(Exhibited in 20 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by the convergence of single-handed operation, critical system failures, and inadequate pre-departure risk management across a range of vessel types and passage profiles. In each incident, the absence of crew redundancy transforms an otherwise manageable technical or navigational problem — engine failure, tiller breakage, adverse conditions — into a situation requiring SAR intervention. The recurring pattern reveals a systemic failure at the pre-departure stage: skippers undertaking solo passages do not consistently demonstrate, nor are they consistently required to demonstrate, that their competency, vessel equipment, and emergency protocols are commensurate with the demands of the intended passage. When the primary system fails, there is no fallback — human, technical, or procedural — to prevent escalation, and SAR becomes the sole safeguard in the risk chain.

### Chemical Exposure Injuries from Inadequate Hazard Controls *(Exhibited in 17 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by crew injuries resulting from chemical exposure — including toxic gas inhalation, chemical burns, eye injuries from spray-back or splash, and product failure — occurring during routine operational or recreational activities aboard fishing vessels, Ro-Ro cargo ships, and service vessels. A consistent pattern emerges in which the handling of hazardous chemical substances proceeds without formal risk assessment, appropriate PPE, or procedural safeguards. Incidents recur because organisational safety cultures permit shortcuts under time pressure or operational routine, deviations from standard equipment and procedures are accepted without management of change processes, and crew hazard awareness training specific to chemical substances remains insufficient. The presence of night-time conditions across multiple incidents amplifies underlying vulnerabilities by reducing visibility, situational awareness, and supervisory oversight, converting nominally low-risk tasks into injury events.

Latent hardware and environmental factors consistently compound procedural and human failures in this theme. Non-standard or defective equipment — including unfamiliar sample bottles, defective snaplights, and unwieldy fuel containers — introduces unexpected failure modes that crews are not prepared to manage. Environmental conditions, particularly adverse sea states, vessel motion, darkness, and confined working spaces, reduce the margin for safe manual handling and increase exposure when chemical containment fails. Human factors including over-familiarity with routine tasks, insufficient training on chemical hazard recognition, and absence of stop-work authority further erode defences. Critically, the absence of engineered controls — gas detection alarms, mandatory PPE enforcement points, formal pre-task briefings, and management of change procedures for third-party equipment — means that individual crew judgement becomes the last and only barrier, an inherently unreliable safeguard in dynamic shipboard environments.

**Representative Cases:**
- **Occurrence e8e395b3-1dad-ed11-83ff-0022481b5dcf**: 2 engineers on a fish carrier inhaled ozone gas. They reported coughing and chest pain. They were provided with medical assistance, prior to that - they relaxed on fresh air.

- **Occurrence f4fb2636-138a-ee11-be36-002248c8714f**: Ro-Ro cargo ship's crew member suffered chemical burns from industrial rust cleaner. Crew member was taken for further medical advice.

### Recreational and Inland Vessel Fires: Systemic Prevention Failures *(Exhibited in 33 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by a recurring pattern of fires, explosions, and carbon monoxide events predominantly aboard recreational, inland waterway, and small commercial vessels, with outcomes ranging from total loss to fatality. The incidents share a common aetiology: latent hardware deficiencies in fuel systems, electrical installations, and engine compartments, operating within a regulatory environment that imposes significantly lower safety standards on recreational craft than on commercial or seagoing vessels. Fire propagation is frequently rapid due to inadequate fixed suppression systems, insufficient vessel spacing in mooring environments, and delayed detection — particularly during night-time operations, which feature prominently across this cluster. Human decision-making compounds physical vulnerabilities, as evidenced by skippers refusing professional assistance, crews remaining aboard compromised vessels under tow, and a broader normalisation of risk that is well-documented in recreational boating culture. The absence of vapour detection, carbon monoxide alarms, and maintained firefighting equipment represents a systemic and largely preventable gap.

Latent hardware failures — including degraded wiring, unserviced fuel lines, and poorly maintained engine compartments — serve as the primary ignition precursors across this cluster, with environmental conditions, particularly night-time darkness and moderate wind, acting as amplifiers that delay detection and accelerate spread rather than as root causes in themselves. Human factors manifest both in pre-incident maintenance neglect and in acute decision-making failures during emergency response, including the override of professional rescue services and inadequate crew training for fire and abandonment scenarios. The structural issue is regulatory: recreational vessels are disproportionately exempt from the inspection regimes, mandatory equipment standards, and crew competency requirements that govern commercial operations. Until safety standards for recreational and inland waterway craft are meaningfully elevated — particularly regarding fire detection, suppression, and crew preparedness — this pattern of preventable incidents will persist.

**Representative Cases:**
- **Occurrence a657b87f-77b9-f011-bbd3-7c1e52038c0e**: News report was passed on reference large fire that engulfed 3 canal boats.
- **Occurrence 9f87f4db-5d3e-f011-877a-0022481ad514**: Motor boat had an explosion. The crew sustained serious injuries and abandoned to the water. The vessel burnt to the waterline and was an actual loss.
- **Occurrence e3b60f9a-791f-f011-998a-002248c611f0**: A fishing vessel experienced an engine room fire. FIRE IN ENGINE ROOM, AUTOMATIC FIRE SURPERSSION SYSTEM ACTIVATED, VESSEL UNABLE TO MANOEUVRE, CURRENTLY ANCHORED, NO INJURIES.

---

### Falls from Ladders: Procedural and Supervisory Failures *(Exhibited in 26 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by crew members sustaining serious injuries — and in one case entering the water — as a result of falls from fixed or portable ladders under conditions that were insufficiently controlled. The incidents span a range of vessel types and tasks, yet share a consistent set of enabling failures: crew members working alone without a buddy or standby person, absence of permit-to-work or working-at-height procedures, and inadequate pre-task risk assessment that fails to account for environmental conditions such as reduced night-time visibility, wind, and the inherent hazard of steep ladder designs. In several cases the outcome was severe or potentially fatal, yet depended heavily on chance factors — a neighbouring vessel, a bystander's discovery — rather than planned safeguards. The recurrence of these incidents across vessel types and operators points not to isolated lapses but to a systemic normalisation of risk, where ladder work is treated as a routine activity rather than a controlled task requiring formal hazard management.

Latent hardware deficiencies compound the procedural failures: ladders that are not secured against movement, absence of anti-slip features, inadequate or absent handrails on steep access ladders, and insufficient fixed lighting on ladder routes all reduce the margin for error available to the crew member. Human factors — including fatigue, possible medical episodes, incorrect body posture (facing away from the ladder), and diminished situational awareness at night — represent the active failure mechanisms, but these are only consequential because the procedural and physical safeguards that should absorb such variability are absent. Environmental conditions, particularly night-time operations and wind-induced instability, consistently appear as compounding factors that elevate residual risk to unacceptable levels. Collectively, these findings indicate that the root cause is a latent failure in safety culture and management system rigour, where the hierarchy of controls for work at height is either not established or not enforced at the operational level.

**Representative Cases:**
- **Occurrence 0efb3786-0f5b-f011-bec1-0022481ad514**: A cargo vessel crew member was on a ladder carrying out a repair, they were on the steps facing away from the ladder when they slipped and fell resulting in a fractured leg. The crew member was passed
- **Occurrence 588780a4-4bd7-ef11-8eea-6045bd0d69a9**: A cargo vessel crew member fell overboard, recovered quickly with no injuries. Crew member slipped from a deck ladder on the outboard side whilst inspecting the contents of the cargo hold on there own
- **Occurrence ae9bfffc-9492-ee11-be37-002248c87a58**: Cargo vessel's crew member was found unconscious at the bottom of a ladder with apparent facial injuries. There were no obvious trip or slip hazards other than the usual steep ladder. The individual c

---

### Crew Injuries During Stairway Transit in Adverse Conditions *(Exhibited in 25 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by crew injuries sustained during routine stairway descent, where commonplace shipboard movement becomes a significant injury risk under the compounding influence of vessel motion, reduced night-time visibility, and behavioural lapses. Across incidents, the immediate causes vary — missed steps, slips, caught fingers, and loss of balance — but a consistent underlying pattern emerges: crew members are transiting stairways without adequate precaution, frequently in the absence of enforced safe movement protocols such as three-point contact or controlled descent speed. The recurrence of these incidents across vessel types, from passenger ships and ro-pax ferries to cargo vessels and dredgers, indicates that stairway transit injury is not an isolated or vessel-specific problem but a systemic safety failure rooted in the normalisation of risk during routine crew movement.

Latent hardware deficiencies play a material role in several incidents, including inadequate anti-slip surfaces, insufficient or poorly positioned handrails, and exposed structural hazards such as proud bulkhead panel seams adjacent to crew transit routes. Environmental conditions — particularly significant wave heights, Beaufort Force 5–6 winds, and night-time lighting limitations — substantially elevate the baseline risk, yet in several cases the recorded sea state data was inconsistent with measured wave heights, suggesting a failure in environmental situational awareness that may have led crews and officers to underestimate prevailing conditions. Human factors, including distraction, excessive descent speed, improper grip posture, and a failure to brace against vessel motion, further compound these vulnerabilities. The convergence of inadequate hardware safeguards, unreliable environmental reporting, and an organisational culture that does not effectively enforce safe movement procedures creates conditions in which injury during a routine transit between decks becomes a foreseeable and preventable outcome.

**Representative Cases:**
- **Occurrence 91f35e8d-5bb7-ed11-b597-0022481b5e4e**: Passenger ship's crew member suffered a knee injury while descending the stairs. This did not result in a fall however, they managed to twist their leg and sprain their knee.  As a consequence of the 
- **Occurrence 32d107e8-38e3-ed11-8846-002248c87a1d**: Hopper dredger's crew member was walking down the stairs from the bridge to the mess room when they slipped and fell down the stairs. This fall resulted in bruised ribs and in an ankle luxation. The c
- **Occurrence 640b4f4d-5a3b-ee11-bdf4-002248c878ca**: A ro-pax crew member caught their finger whilst descending a staircase resulting in a fracture and subsequent sign off from the vessel. There were no witnesses to the incident but investigations carri

### Exclusion Zone Failures During Cargo Handling Operations *(Exhibited in 13 incidents)*

Latent hardware deficiencies — including the absence of tag lines for suspended load control, missing banksmen equipment, and unsecured loads on forklifts — removed the last physical safeguards against injury once human and environmental factors aligned adversely. Human factors, particularly complacency toward habitual tasks, inadequate situational awareness (compounded in several cases by carrying loads that obstructed sightlines), and operational pressure to sustain cargo schedules, consistently overrode risk perception. Environmental conditions — night-time lighting, moderate sea states inducing vessel motion, and wind speeds sufficient to cause pallet swing — acted as force multipliers on already degraded procedural controls. The convergence of these latent conditions means that injury was structurally embedded in the way these operations were being conducted, and only systemic intervention at the procedural and supervisory level will interrupt the pattern.

### Struck-By and Trip Hazards in Confined Workspaces *(Exhibited in 8 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by crew injuries resulting from contact with fixed overhead obstructions, unsecured equipment, and cluttered deck spaces within confined or restricted working environments aboard vessels. The incidents share a common latent failure: the working environment had not been systematically assessed to identify physical hazards — whether low-clearance pipework and sprinkler heads, unsecured pallets in alleyways, or stowed equipment left in fall zones — before crew were required to work within or transit through those spaces. Across all cases, no compensatory controls such as hazard marking, physical guarding, pre-task briefings, or enforced housekeeping standards were in place. The recurring nature of these incidents points to a systemic gap in routine risk assessment and safety culture, whereby the acceptance of cluttered, poorly marked, or physically hazardous working spaces has become normalised aboard affected vessels.

Latent hardware factors are significant throughout this theme: fixed firefighting system components installed at crew head height, pipework at low clearance, and the inherent spatial constraints of shaft tunnels and engine rooms create environments where the margin for safe movement is minimal and unforgiving. Human factors — including reduced situational awareness, inattention to footing, and unfamiliarity with specific spatial hazards — consistently amplify the underlying physical risks, particularly during transitional movements such as stepping up, standing from a crouched position, or walking through a passageway. Night-time working conditions compound these deficiencies across multiple incidents by degrading visual detection of obstructions and overhead hazards. The convergence of unguarded fixed hazards, inadequate housekeeping discipline, absent or unenforced procedural controls, and degraded environmental conditions reveals a systemic failure in how physical workspace risks are identified, communicated, and mitigated as part of routine shipboard safety management.

**Representative Cases:**
- **Occurrence 56e29e30-6ee5-ef11-9342-6045bd0dbe09**: A replenishment tanker crew member was working in a machinery space shaft tunnel with low head room. As the crew member went to step up onto the floor plating, their head brushed a fixed firefighting 
- **Occurrence c5c6884a-c0fb-ed11-8f6d-002248c87a40**: A ro-pax ferry crewmember had just exited the lift and was walking down an alleyway, when they tripped over an unused pallet that had been left there. The crewmember put out their right hand to break 
- **Occurrence c829033e-3374-f011-b4cc-002248c61a33**: An offshore support vessel crewmember was working in the engine room when they overbalanced and fell backwards. They struck a storage box and sustained grazes to their arm and shin. The crewmember was

### Engine Failure and Deficient Emergency Preparedness in Small Vessels *(Exhibited in 16 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by propulsion system failures aboard small recreational and commercial motor vessels that escalate into search and rescue incidents due to an absence of effective emergency response measures, inadequate safety equipment, and poor pre-departure preparation. Across these incidents, engine failure — whether caused by mechanical fault, water ingress, fuel issues, or deferred maintenance — consistently serves as the proximate trigger. However, the recurring and preventable nature of these incidents points to deeper systemic deficiencies: crews departing without mandatory safety equipment including lifejackets and functioning communication devices, no redundant propulsion or anchoring capability, and an apparent absence of formalised pre-departure safety checks. The pattern of non-compliance with basic recreational and commercial vessel safety requirements indicates a broader safety culture deficit in which established maritime protocols are neither consistently enforced nor internalised by vessel operators.

Latent hardware deficiencies — including poorly maintained outboard engines, water ingress into engine compartments, and absent or unverified anchoring equipment — remove the vessel's primary means of propulsion and station-keeping at the moment they are most needed. These mechanical vulnerabilities are compounded by human factors including low situational awareness, absence of emergency drill preparedness, and inadequate crew medical risk management, which collectively prevent effective mitigation once a failure occurs. Environmental conditions, particularly night-time operations and moderate sea states, further reduce the margin for safe self-rescue and increase the complexity of SAR response. The convergence of these latent hardware failures, procedural non-compliance, and environmental exposure transforms individually manageable deficiencies into multi-agency SAR events, and in more adverse conditions could readily result in loss of life.

**Representative Cases:**
- **Occurrence 09939c3a-9a58-f011-bec2-0022481a4e86**: A motor vessel had an engine failure and subsequently struck a pier. The vessel with two crew onboard was holed above the waterline with no water ingress. Only one crew member was wearing a lifejacket
- **Occurrence 771c1f6f-7c05-f011-bae2-0022481ad514**: A member of the public phoned to report a recreational fishing vessel that was seen drifting appox. 200m from shore. The four crew onboard were not wearing lifejackets, it was unknown if the vessel ha
- **Occurrence 507c67cb-c616-f011-998a-6045bd0cc1f6**: A motorsailer crew member sustained a head injury, due to communications being poor and the crew member having a medical condition a search and rescue (SAR) vessel was tasked to assist. On arrival it

The incidents span both fishing vessels and general cargo vessels, occur predominantly at night, and consistently involve moderate but operationally significant environmental conditions. The pattern indicates that these are not isolated mechanical misfortunes but rather systemic failures in which the primary safeguard (propulsion) and the secondary safeguard (emergency anchoring) fail in sequence, leaving the vessel entirely reliant on third-party SAR intervention or fortuitous circumstances to avoid catastrophic outcome. The fatal convergence of these failures in proximity to shallow water, rocky reefs, or a falling tide amplifies consequence severity markedly.\n\nThe latent hardware dimension is central to this theme: propeller defects, engine faults, and anchor system inadequacies — in terms of weight, type, scope deployed, or seabed compatibility — consistently go undetected through pre-departure or routine maintenance inspections, suggesting a deficient maintenance culture and inspection regime. Human factors compound this latent vulnerability; crews operating alone or in small teams at night demonstrate reduced situational awareness of hazard proximity and face heightened cognitive load when managing simultaneous emergency tasks such as re-starting the engine, deploying anchors, and transmitting distress signals. Environmental conditions, while not extreme, are operationally significant: wind speeds in the 16–20 kph range and wave heights around 1.0–1.5 metres are sufficient to overwhelm an inadequately sized or poorly deployed anchor, accelerate drift toward coastal hazards, and reduce the effectiveness of SAR towing operations. The absence of onboard contingency tow arrangements and the lack of formal emergency procedures for propulsion loss near hazardous coastal features further reflect a systemic gap in pre-departure risk assessment and emergency preparedness planning.",
  "solas_chapters": [

### Engine Compartment Fires and Smoke Misidentification at Night *(Exhibited in 35 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by mechanical failures within engine compartments — including gearbox seal degradation, exhaust system faults, lubrication breakdown in auxiliary machinery, and ignition of combustible insulation materials — that either produce genuine fires or generate smoke misidentified as fire events. The recurring nature of these incidents reflects a systemic pattern of undetected fault conditions developing over time, driven by inadequate preventive maintenance regimes and insufficient inspection frequency on propulsion and auxiliary equipment. In several cases, the consequences were compounded by crew responses disproportionate to the actual hazard, with unnecessary emergency activations consuming harbour and lifeboat resources. The common thread is not a single point of failure but rather a latent accumulation of deferred maintenance, material degradation, and missed pre-departure inspection opportunities that allow manageable technical deficiencies to escalate into emergency situations.

Latent hardware deficiencies — such as degraded seals, cracked exhaust manifolds, substandard insulation materials, and overdue servicing of auxiliary equipment — represent the primary preconditions for these incidents. These hardware vulnerabilities are compounded by human factor deficits, most notably insufficient crew familiarity with engine compartment layouts, exhaust system behaviour, and the diagnostic skills required to distinguish between a fire and a mechanical smoke-producing fault. Environmental conditions, particularly night-time operations with reduced ambient lighting, consistently impaired crew ability to accurately assess smoke source and nature, accelerating escalation to emergency response. Taken together, these factors illustrate that neither hardware reliability, crew training, nor environmental awareness alone is sufficient; effective prevention requires an integrated approach addressing maintenance quality, crew diagnostic competency, and the provision of adequate lighting and detection technology within engine spaces.

### Engine Room Fires: Latent Defects and Unmanned Vessel Risk *(Exhibited in 26 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by fires originating in machinery spaces, driven by latent mechanical or electrical deficiencies — including fuel line degradation, lubrication failures, electrical faults, and inadequate heat shielding — that remain undetected until ignition conditions are reached. A recurring and critical systemic failure is the unmanned status of vessels at the time of ignition, which eliminates the possibility of early human detection and intervention, allowing fires to escalate unchecked. Where crew are present, night operations, moderate to rough sea states, and reduced visibility compound the difficulty of early detection and effective firefighting response. The pattern across these incidents demonstrates that neither routine inspection regimes nor onboard emergency preparedness are sufficient to address the underlying vulnerability: a latent defect that reaches ignition threshold in the absence of adequate monitoring, detection, or suppression infrastructure.

Latent hardware deficiencies represent the primary precondition across this cluster, with fuel and oil systems, electrical installations, and high-temperature engine components collectively forming the most common ignition pathways. These defects are rarely self-evident during standard operational checks, and the absence of structured pre-departure engine room shutdown inspections — particularly before vessels are left unmanned — allows hazardous conditions to persist unrecognised. Human factors manifest principally as procedural gaps: the absence of ventilation and gas detection protocols prior to opening engine compartments, insufficient pre-departure machinery space inspections, and inadequate pollution prevention discipline during emergency response. Environmental conditions, including night operations and elevated sea states, act as compounding factors that increase mechanical stress on propulsion systems and degrade crew situational awareness. The cascading consequences observed — loss of propulsion, water ingress, vessel sinking, mooring failures causing secondary damage, and crew injury — confirm that single-point failures in engine room fire prevention and detection architecture carry the potential for catastrophic, multi-system outcomes.

- **Occurrence 52a4e245-7684-ee11-8179-002248c87a1d**: A crew transfer vessel experienced propulsion failure following a fire and subsequent water ingress. The crew on board controlled the fire and extinguished it using the vessel's fire suppression syste

### Recreational Activity Injuries Aboard Passenger Vessels at Night *(Exhibited in 16 incidents)*

This theme encompasses injuries sustained by cruise ship passengers during onboard recreational activities — principally court sports such as basketball and table tennis, and social activities such as dancing — occurring predominantly under night-time conditions. The cluster is characterised by a convergence of dynamic physical activity, reduced environmental awareness, and an absence of formalised risk management frameworks governing passenger leisure spaces. Incidents recur because cruise operators treat recreational areas as amenity spaces rather than operationally managed risk environments, resulting in the absence of sea-state-dependent activity restrictions, inadequate pre-participation safety briefings, and limited supervision. The inherently dynamic nature of court sports and social dancing introduces foreseeable fall and collision hazards that are materially elevated aboard a moving vessel, yet procedural controls commensurate with that elevated risk are consistently absent across the incidents examined.

Latent hardware deficiencies — including unverified non-slip deck surfaces, the placement of obstructions such as speakers within or adjacent to activity areas, and the adequacy of perimeter barriers and fall-arrest features — represent a persistent physical risk layer that is rarely formally assessed. Human factors, including diminished spatial awareness and degraded reaction capability under night-time lighting, compounded by the close physical proximity inherent to multi-player sports, consistently elevate injury probability beyond what would be expected in equivalent onshore environments. Environmental conditions, particularly sea states ranging from slight to rough, introduce vessel motion that further compromises player balance and stability on recreational courts. Taken together, these latent factors illustrate a systemic failure to apply structured safety oversight to passenger recreational activities, with operators relying on passive acceptance of risk rather than proactive hazard mitigation — a gap that has resulted in injuries ranging from ligament ruptures and fractures to, in one case, a fatality following a fall on the dance floor.

### Navigational Misjudgement During High-Risk Confined Transits *(Exhibited in 20 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by navigational errors occurring during high-risk, spatially constrained manoeuvres — principally bridge transits, platform approaches, and pilotage in restricted waters — where officers or pilots misjudge clearances, issue incorrect helm orders, or become distracted from their primary watchkeeping duties. A recurring pattern across incidents is the absence of structured procedural frameworks specific to these manoeuvres: no formal bridge transit protocols, no platform approach checklists, and no enforced helm order verification procedures. This procedural vacuum means that individual human error — whether a momentary lapse in situational awareness, a miscommunicated helm order, or distraction by an administrative task — is not intercepted by any systemic safeguard, allowing minor misjudgements to escalate into contacts, groundings, or crew injuries. The frequency of these incidents suggests an organisational tolerance for ad hoc navigation practices during phases that objectively demand the highest level of procedural discipline.

Latent hardware and environmental conditions consistently reduce the margin available to detect and recover from human error. Night-time operations appear as a recurring environmental factor, degrading visual situational awareness precisely when accurate perception of clearances and vessel positioning is most critical. Moderate wind and sea state further reduce manoeuvrability and helm responsiveness, compressing the time available for corrective action. Hardware deficiencies — including the absence of active ECDIS guard zones, proximity alarms, and adequate bow fendering — mean that vessels lack automated early-warning capability and physical resilience during close-quarters work. The combination of degraded environmental conditions, absent automation safeguards, and procedural gaps creates a predictable latent failure pathway: the officer or pilot is the sole, unsupported barrier against contact or grounding, and when that barrier fails, no secondary defence exists to prevent the incident or mitigate its consequences.

- **Occurrence 5da745d3-155d-ee11-8def-002248c878ca**: The vessel's second mate had just taken over the watch. The was no current work available to do so the vessel was on normal standby and was required to carry out cargo operations at a local platform.

### Wind-Induced Loss of Control During Berthing Operations *(Exhibited in 36 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by vessels losing directional control during slow-speed berthing or close-quarters manoeuvring as a result of wind forces exceeding the vessel's controllability envelope, particularly under night-time conditions. Across these incidents, a consistent pattern emerges whereby operators and pilots either failed to adequately assess prevailing meteorological conditions prior to commencing the manoeuvre, or possessed the information but did not integrate it into a revised berthing strategy. The recurrence of these incidents is driven by a systemic absence of defined wind speed thresholds or mandatory abort criteria within berthing protocols, meaning that decisions to proceed in marginal or deteriorating conditions are left to individual judgement without procedural safeguard. Compounding factors include discrepancies between officially reported sea states and actual recorded wave heights, suggesting a cultural reliance on forecast data rather than real-time environmental observation, and the reduced spatial awareness inherent to night-time operations.

From a latent causation perspective, hardware limitations — including insufficient thruster authority at low berthing speeds and the absence of deployed fenders, mooring lines, or tug assistance — significantly reduced the margin of control available to crews once wind-induced drift commenced. Human factors are pervasive across this theme: failures of situational awareness, inadequate pre-manoeuvre risk assessment, and reactive decision-making under time pressure in confined port environments collectively eroded the safety margin before contact occurred. Environmental conditions, particularly wind speeds in the 21–23 kph range and reduced night-time visibility, were rarely individually catastrophic but consistently served as the trigger that exposed underlying procedural and planning deficiencies. The presence of concurrent vessel movements in confined port areas further narrowed the decision space available to pilots, transforming manageable situations into unrecoverable ones. Taken together, these incidents reflect a latent systemic failure: the absence of formalised environmental risk assessment requirements embedded within berthing and port entry procedures.

- **Occurrence e8dcbaf5-21c9-f011-8544-6045bdd1a463**: A search and rescue vessel was approaching a berth in preparation to be lifted out of the water for maintenance, when it struck the concrete ledge of the berth. This resulted in damage to handrails on
- **Occurrence 345050ae-52b7-ed11-b596-0022481b5ab5**: Cargo vessel was coming alongside the passenger ship when it came into contact with the passenger ship's port quarter belting. This resulted in damage to the cargo vessel's side railings, bulwark and 
- **Occurrence 7c40c62c-d0bd-ed11-83fe-0022481b977e**: An offshore supply vessel was under pilotage and maneuvering in a port area, when the pilot took action to avoid another offshore supply vessel. During the maneuver the pilot reportedly lost control w

### Propulsion Failure Due to Latent Machinery Maintenance Deficiencies *(Exhibited in 28 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by complete or partial loss of propulsion arising from undetected mechanical or electrical degradation within vessel power plants, cooling systems, and associated drivetrain components. Across the incident set, a consistent pattern emerges whereby developing hardware faults — including solenoid wear, cooling system failures, oil leaks, and electrical system degradation — were not identified prior to departure or during routine operations, ultimately manifesting as total propulsion loss at sea or in navigationally sensitive areas. The recurrence of this pattern across vessel types ranging from small fishing vessels to general cargo ships and motor pleasure craft indicates a systemic failure of maintenance culture and regulatory oversight rather than isolated random mechanical events. The consequences were largely mitigated by favourable environmental conditions, proximity to assistance, and effective crew responses; however, the operational outcomes were largely fortuitous rather than the product of robust risk management.

Latent hardware degradation is the primary causal driver across this theme, with procedural deficiencies serving as the enabling condition that allows such degradation to progress undetected to the point of operational failure. The absence of documented planned maintenance systems, inadequate pre-departure machinery checks, and the lack of predictive component lifecycle management collectively prevent early identification of developing faults. Human factors, while less prominent in isolation, are embedded in the procedural failures — specifically in the normalisation of deferred maintenance and insufficient watchkeeping vigilance for early warning indicators such as abnormal temperatures, pressures, or fluid levels. Environmental conditions, including night-time operations, moderate sea states, port approaches, and isolated waters, did not cause these incidents but materially increased their consequence severity by reducing the margin for safe recovery once propulsion was lost. Addressing this theme requires structural improvement in maintenance governance, particularly for smaller commercial vessel classes where planned maintenance system requirements and enforcement remain inconsistent.

**Representative Cases:**
- **Occurrence 1747e924-4eb8-ed11-b597-0022481b50d1**: A fishing vessel experienced power failure and was towed back to port. The vessel was at anchor so was not in immediate danger. Another fishing vessel stood by until the lifeboat arrived and towed the
- **Occurrence 71cefc45-a01a-f011-998a-002248c61a33**: A general cargo vessel experienced propulsion failure during departure from a port with a pilot on board. The pilot requested permission to anchor in the channel. The port also arranged a tug to stand
- **Occurrence 557be3b5-2168-ee11-9ae7-002248c87a58**: A potter fishing vessel suffered propulsion failure and called for assistance. Another fishing vessel provided assistance in the form of a tow back to harbour. There were no injuries, pollution or dam

### Propulsion Failure Due to Latent Machinery Maintenance Deficiencies *(Exhibited in 8 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by the catastrophic failure of main propulsion systems in fishing vessels, arising primarily from undetected or neglected deficiencies in engine cooling systems and associated machinery. Across all incidents in this cluster, the common thread is an absence of structured, documented preventative maintenance regimes that would otherwise identify developing faults — such as degraded sea water pump impellers, cooling system leaks, or blocked sea strainers — before they escalate to total propulsion loss. The recurrence of these incidents reflects a systemic reliance on informal maintenance practices prevalent in small-scale fishing vessel operations, where structured safety management systems are rarely implemented and pre-voyage machinery checks are either cursory or undocumented. The consequential loss of propulsion in open waters, frequently at night and in adverse sea conditions, then triggers a cascade of secondary risks including prolonged drifting, dangerous towing operations, and inadequate communication of the vessel's distress.

### Fuel System Contamination and Maintenance Failures Causing Propulsion Loss *(Exhibited in 11 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by recurring propulsion failures attributable to contaminated or degraded fuel systems, encompassing blocked fuel filters, injector pump deficiencies, and the absence of effective onboard fuel treatment processes. Across all incidents, a consistent pattern emerges: contaminated fuel — whether introduced during bunkering, transferred from poorly managed day tanks, or accumulated through degraded system components — reaches critical engine fuel systems without being intercepted by adequate filtration, purification, or pre-departure inspection regimes. These failures are systemic rather than isolated, reflecting organisational shortcomings in preventive maintenance scheduling, bunkering quality assurance, and crew competency in emergency fuel system recovery procedures. The repeated dependency on external towing assistance further underscores the operational impact of these deficiencies, particularly when they manifest in vessels carrying passengers or operating in exposed waters.

Latent hardware deficiencies — including worn injector pump seals, degraded filter media, and inoperative centrifugal separators — form the physical foundation of these incidents, but their occurrence is enabled by deeper human and procedural failures. Maintenance intervals are frequently inadequate or unenforced, pre-departure engineering checks are either absent or insufficiently rigorous, and crew members in several cases lacked the practical competency to execute fuel system recovery procedures such as bleeding after filter replacement. Environmental conditions, particularly night-time operations and sea states of force 5 or above, consistently compounded the consequences by impairing crew situational awareness, delaying detection of early warning signs, and increasing navigational risk during the loss-of-propulsion period. The convergence of these latent procedural gaps, hardware vulnerabilities, and adverse environmental context transforms what should be detectable, preventable maintenance failures into high-risk casualty events.

- **Occurrence 589e6798-104b-ee11-be6f-002248c87a58**: Charter vessel reported an engine failure which resulted in a tow. It was noticed by an engineer that the fuel line and filters were fully blocked. This issue has been resolved and no injuries were su
- **Occurrence 814333da-2de9-eb11-bacb-000d3a86d165**: A leisure fishing vessel had engine failure and fuel leaking into the starboard bilge. The fuel leaked from the injector pump and started to fill the starboard bilge with red diesel. The vessel was to

### Recreational Vessel Propulsion Failure and Inadequate Emergency Preparedness *(Exhibited in 10 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by recreational and small commercial vessels experiencing propulsion system failures — encompassing engine, gearbox, battery, and drivetrain degradation — that render the vessel unable to manoeuvre, typically at night and in coastal waters proximate to navigational hazards. The recurring nature of these incidents reflects a systemic pattern rather than isolated equipment misfortune: vessels are departing without adequate pre-departure mechanical checks, without verified emergency equipment readiness, and without crew possessing the seamanship competency to execute secondary contingencies such as anchor deployment. The consequence in each case is an escalation from a recoverable equipment failure to a Search and Rescue tasking, with SAR resources bearing the operational burden of risk that was generated ashore, before departure.

Latent hardware deficiencies — undetected gearbox wear, battery degradation, and fuel or drivetrain faults — form the proximate mechanical cause across this cluster, but their significance is compounded by the consistent absence of effective maintenance regimes and pre-departure inspection practices capable of identifying developing faults before sea. Human factors, including gaps in seamanship knowledge and failure to deploy anchors as a station-keeping measure while awaiting assistance, indicate that crew preparedness is a critical and undertreated vulnerability. Environmental conditions, specifically night-time operation in moderate wind and swell, systematically elevated the consequence of each mechanical failure by reducing situational awareness, accelerating drift toward hazards, and complicating SAR coordination. The combination of latent hardware degradation, procedural deficiency, and adverse operating environment created conditions in which foreseeable and preventable failures produced serious SAR incidents.

- **Occurrence 6d2abc33-d64d-f011-877b-002248c61a33**: A recreational vessel drifted towards rock, search and rescue towed vessel to safety. Search and rescue were tasked to assist a motor vessel that was drifting towards rocks with 3 POB. SAR took vessel
- **Occurrence a9a898c4-f0b1-ed11-83ff-0022481b58fd**: A recreational motor cruiser experienced propulsion failure. The vessel dropped anchor and called for assistance. Initially a commercial vessel offered their services but subsequently were unable to r
- **Occurrence 364a6415-8614-f011-998a-002248c68fab**: A recreational fishing vessel with two crew onboard reported a gearbox failure, the vessel was drifting as the anchor was unable to be deployed. Both crew were wearing lifejackets. A search and rescue

### Propulsion Failure in Adverse Conditions, Single-Engine Vessels *(Exhibited in 58 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by the catastrophic convergence of mechanical propulsion failure and hazardous operating conditions aboard recreational and small commercial vessels. The majority of incidents (53 machinery-related) involve single-engine vessels that lose all motive power, leaving them beam-on to wind and sea, adrift in shipping lanes, or subject to progressive flooding risk. These incidents recur because operators routinely accept the latent vulnerability of single-engine configurations without adequate compensatory measures — no redundant propulsion, no functioning sailing backup, and no credible contingency plan. Compounding factors such as night-time departure, solo operation, and unseaworthy vessel condition transform what might be a manageable mechanical deficiency into a life-threatening emergency requiring external search and rescue intervention. The decision to operate in deteriorating weather or at night without verified propulsion reliability is a consistent antecedent across this cluster.

The latent hardware failures at the root of these incidents — undetected fuel contamination, deferred maintenance, worn or neglected engine components, and overall vessel disrepair — reflect a systemic failure of pre-departure safety culture rather than isolated mechanical misfortune. Human factors including overconfidence, normalisation of deferred maintenance, and inadequate voyage planning are pervasive, with operators frequently departing without verifying that primary or backup systems are serviceable. Environmental conditions, particularly night-time sea states of Sea State 3–4, moderate winds, cold temperatures, and swell, do not cause these incidents but act as force multipliers that compress the time available for self-rescue and dramatically increase the severity of crew harm, as evidenced by cases of hypothermia and beam-on rolling risk. The consistent absence of adequate anchoring capability, working sailing rigs, or secondary propulsion means that once the engine fails, the vessel and crew are entirely dependent on external SAR resources, creating preventable risk to both survivors and rescuers.

### Passenger Falls From Unmitigated Deck-Level Trip Hazards *(Exhibited in 12 incidents)*

This theme characterises a recurring pattern of passenger injuries resulting from trips and falls on low-profile, deck-level hazards — primarily sun lounger legs and fitness equipment structural bases — in high-traffic recreational areas aboard cruise vessels. These incidents share a common latent profile: portable or semi-permanent equipment is positioned in pedestrian zones without adequate hazard assessment, demarcation, or procedural controls, and this risk is compounded during night-time operations and moderate sea states when visibility is reduced and vessel motion impairs passenger balance and stability. The consistent recurrence of these injuries across multiple vessel areas and conditions indicates that the hazard is systemic rather than incidental, and that reactive attribution of causation solely to passenger inattention forecloses the identification and correction of underlying shipboard safety management failures.

The latent conditions driving this theme operate across all three causal domains. From a hardware perspective, sun lounger wheels and protruding structural bases present low-profile, visually inconspicuous hazards that are inherently difficult for passengers to detect, particularly at night or when attention is divided. Environmentally, sea states generating vessel motion — even at moderate levels — materially increase the biomechanical difficulty of maintaining balance while walking, and night-time lighting standards on open decks frequently prove inadequate to illuminate deck-level obstructions. Human factors are present both at the passenger level, where distraction and unfamiliarity with equipment layout reduce situational awareness, and at the organisational level, where the absence of structured safety briefings for fitness equipment users and the lack of formalised procedures for reviewing and adjusting deck furniture arrangements in response to sea conditions and reduced visibility represent failures of safety management. Collectively, these factors create a foreseeable injury pathway that should be addressed through proactive design, procedural, and operational controls rather than post-incident attribution.
