### Passenger Trip Hazards from Inadequate Deck Safety Management *(Exhibited in 8 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by a recurring failure to manage foreseeable deck-level trip hazards in areas accessed by passengers during embarkation, transit, and disembarkation. Across all incidents, a common latent organisational deficiency is evident: the absence of enforced procedural controls — such as post-lashing stowage requirements, pre-passenger-access deck inspections, vulnerability-based passenger assistance protocols, and systematic risk assessment of vessel-to-shore transition interfaces — that would otherwise identify and mitigate hazards before passenger exposure. The hazards themselves, whether unsecured restraint chains, vessel-to-pontoon gaps, or fixed deck transition strips, are either created through routine operational activity or are persistent features that have been normalised through repeated use without formal review. The consequence is that foreseeable, preventable injuries continue to occur because no robust safety management layer exists between the hazard and the passenger.

Latent hardware deficiencies — including inadequate gangway or bridging plate provision, raised deck transition strips, and hazardous pontoon surface materials — create the physical conditions for injury, but these are consistently compounded by procedural and human factor failures. Crew members responsible for lashing operations do not stow equipment clear of pedestrian routes, and no supervisory or inspection mechanism exists to catch this omission before passengers are admitted to the space. The absence of targeted assistance protocols for mobility-impaired or otherwise vulnerable passengers further increases the consequence severity when hazards are encountered. In all cases, night-time environmental conditions reduced passenger visibility of deck-level hazards, demonstrating that existing risk assessments have not adequately accounted for the interaction between lighting conditions, vessel movement, and passenger vulnerability. Collectively, these incidents reflect a systemic gap in the application of the safety management system to routine passenger-facing operations.

The recurring nature of these incidents suggests that no single discrete failure is responsible; rather, they arise from the convergence of environmental conditions — reduced visibility at night and low-amplitude vessel motion — with individual human factors such as unfamiliarity with shipboard movement, inappropriate footwear, and divided attention. Critically, post-incident inspections consistently found no physical defects, which risks masking the true hazard: the stairway environment as designed may be adequate under static, daytime conditions but inadequate under the dynamic operating conditions that are routinely encountered in service.

The latent hardware dimension centres on stairway design elements — step nosing visibility, handrail continuity, tread grip, and localised step lighting — which may meet baseline standards yet fail to provide sufficient compensation when vessel motion and low-light conditions interact. Human factors include passengers' inherent inability to anticipate or adapt to shipboard dynamics without prior experience or adequate briefing, compounded by the absence of consistent, operationally specific safety communication regarding staircase use in variable conditions. Procedurally, the evidence suggests that passenger safety briefings do not reliably address context-specific risks such as night-time movement or staircase use in sea states above calm. The fatality within this cluster underscores that what is routinely classified as a minor or less serious event category carries a potential severity that existing preventive measures are not reliably mitigating.

### Passenger Bathroom Slip Hazards in Marine Environments *(Exhibited in 14 incidents)*

This theme encompasses a recurring pattern of passenger slip, trip, and fall incidents within cruise ship cabin bathrooms, predominantly occurring in shower areas and at shower exit points. The incidents are characterised by wet floor surfaces, inadequate or absent fixed anti-slip provisions, and unsecured or bypassed bathmats in a confined, dynamic marine environment. A common thread across all incidents is the absence of effective passive safety infrastructure — specifically fixed non-slip floor treatments and appropriately positioned grab rails — combined with an over-reliance on moveable soft furnishings such as towel bathmats as the primary, and in many cases sole, slip-prevention measure. These incidents recur not because of isolated acts of passenger carelessness, but because the vessel's safety management system has failed to recognise unsecured bathmats and unprotected wet floor areas as foreseeable, systemic hazards requiring active mitigation in the marine cabin environment.

Latent hardware deficiencies are central to this theme: bathroom design frequently lacks fixed anti-slip surface treatments, secondary grab rails at shower exit points, and appropriately positioned support structures for passengers with mobility limitations. Human factors compound these deficiencies, as passengers — unfamiliar with vessel motion and without adequate safety briefings or cabin literature — make uninformed decisions such as foregoing available bathmats or showering without adequate support. Environmental conditions, including sea states ranging from slight to moderate and night-time operation, further elevate risk by introducing vessel motion and reducing passenger situational awareness in an already hazardous wet space. The post-incident finding of no defects in multiple cases reveals a systemic failure in hazard identification: the vessel's defect reporting and safety management frameworks do not adequately classify soft furnishing placement, fixed anti-slip provision, or grab rail adequacy as auditable safety-critical items, allowing the latent risk to persist undetected and unaddressed across the fleet.

### RIB Wave-Impact Injuries: Night Operations and Passenger Safety *(Exhibited in 11 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by spinal and musculoskeletal injuries sustained by passengers and crew aboard Rigid Inflatable Boats (RIBs) as a direct consequence of wave-induced slamming forces transmitted through the rigid hull to inadequately supported or unbraced occupants. The incidents cluster around a consistent failure pattern: commercial RIB operations conducted at night or in dynamic sea conditions without formal speed management protocols, structured passenger safety briefings, or adequate seat retention systems. Notably, injuries occur even in relatively modest sea states, indicating that vessel speed relative to conditions — rather than absolute wave height — is often the primary injury mechanism. The bow seating position is disproportionately exposed to vertical shock loading, yet passengers are routinely seated there without instruction on bracing posture or forewarning of the physical demands of RIB passage in open water.

Latent hardware deficiencies are a persistent underlying factor, with RIB seating configurations frequently lacking shock-mitigating suspension, grab handles, or restraint systems appropriate for the dynamic loads generated during commercial passenger operations. Human factors compound these shortcomings: operators demonstrate a systemic absence of pre-departure safety briefings tailored to RIB-specific hazards, and there is little evidence of formalised speed-reduction thresholds tied to sea state, visibility, or operational lighting conditions. Environmental conditions — including night-time operation that reduces the operator's ability to anticipate wave strikes, ship wakes generating sudden localised disturbance in otherwise calm water, and wind-driven chop — interact with these latent failures to produce foreseeable and preventable injury events. The recurrence of this pattern across multiple operators and contexts suggests a sector-wide normalisation of risk that requires both regulatory attention and industry-level procedural reform.

**Representative Cases:**
- **Occurrence f8d479fe-850e-ee11-8f6e-002248c87a58**: RIB's crew member sustained a back injury after the RIB caught a wave. The casualty has been taken for further medical assistance.

The recurrence of these incidents is attributable not to singular failures but to systemic latent deficiencies in operational risk management — specifically, the lack of formalised speed-management protocols relative to sea state, insufficient passenger seating allocation procedures, and an organisational culture that normalises operating without structured passenger safety preparation in active sea conditions.\n\nThe latent hardware dimension is evident in suspension seats not adjusted to occupant weight, RIB bow geometries susceptible to stuffing in following seas, and the absence of documented spray protection or motion-dampening provisions. Human factors are central: helmsmen and coxswains demonstrating inadequate speed reduction in deteriorating or following sea conditions, crew failing to intervene when passengers adopt biomechanically hazardous postures, and operators conducting no dynamic risk assessment prior to or during passages in night-time or rough conditions. Environmental conditions — including night-time operations that remove visual anticipation of incoming wave sets, sea states of 3 to 5 generating significant vertical vessel acceleration, and following seas creating bow-stuffing risk — compound these latent failures. Individually, none of these factors is necessarily causal; in combination, they create an injury environment that is both predictable and preventable.",

### Staircase Fall Injuries Due to Missed Final Step *(Exhibited in 29 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by passengers and crew members sustaining serious injuries — including fractured limbs and knee injuries — as a result of failing to perceive or correctly negotiate the final step of a staircase or a single step change in a walking surface. A consistent pattern across incidents is the absence of any formally identified physical defect following post-incident inspection, which creates a dangerous illusion of compliance. In reality, these incidents recur because passive hazard mitigation measures — including step edge contrast markings, nosing visibility, and ambient lighting levels — are assessed under daytime or controlled inspection conditions that do not replicate the degraded perceptual environment experienced by users at night or during vessel motion. The gap between inspection standards and real operational conditions represents a systemic latent risk that current reporting and inspection frameworks fail to reliably surface.

Latent hardware deficiencies, particularly insufficient step-edge contrast markings and suboptimal staircase lighting design, reduce the perceptual salience of the final step transition under night-time conditions — the precise scenario most frequently associated with these incidents. Moderate sea states and vessel motion compound the risk by impairing passenger balance and stability during descent, while hard deck surfaces such as marble increase the severity of injury upon impact. Human factors, including inattention, depth perception error, and unfamiliarity with shipboard movement, interact with these environmental and design deficiencies to produce falls. The delayed reporting observed in several incidents further exacerbates medical outcomes. The absence of passenger safety briefings or targeted hazard signage at known risk locations — particularly staircases during night hours or adverse sea conditions — indicates a broader procedural gap in the vessel's safety communication strategy.

**Representative Cases:**
- **Occurrence 44d04291-9bb6-ed11-b597-6045bd0b12c1**: Passenger ship guest  was walking when they failed to notice a step ahead which resulted in them tripping and losing their balance. This resulted in a fractured upper arm. Location was inspected and n
- **Occurrence f8b40820-9ed1-ef11-8ee9-6045bd0d69a9**: A cruise ship passenger fell when they misjudged the last step on the staircase and fell to the deck, resulting in a fractured ankle. The injury was not reported immediately and the passenger did not 
- **Occurrence 6c2e437a-d0f2-ef11-be20-0022481a4e86**: A cruise ship passenger was descending a staircase when they missed the last step resulting in them to lose their balance, and fall forwards onto the marble floor landing on their right knee. They wer

### Mobility Aid Misuse and Passenger Vulnerability Risks Aboard *(Exhibited in 20 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by a convergence of improvised passenger behaviour, unregulated mobility aid operation, and the absence of structured vulnerability assessment processes. Incidents in this cluster recur because vessels lack systematic mechanisms to identify mobility-impaired or at-risk passengers and to translate that identification into targeted supervision, physical safeguards, or behavioural guidance. Companions and passengers themselves routinely improvise solutions to mobility challenges — pushing walkers as wheelchairs, operating scooters in restricted areas, or performing routine movements without seeking assistance — in ways that are foreseeable yet unaddressed by any formal shipboard procedure or environmental control. The shipboard setting amplifies these risks through sloped decks, abrupt flooring transitions, open deck edges, and vessel movement, converting low-consequence shore-side behaviours into serious or fatal injury events.

Latent hardware deficiencies — including wheeled mobility aids without effective braking, insufficient physical barriers at open deck edges, and abrupt mixed-surface flooring transitions — create an environment in which a single lapse of control or judgement produces disproportionate consequences. Human factors, including underestimation of personal risk, unfamiliarity with vessel dynamics, and the instinctive desire of companions to assist without formal guidance, sustain a pattern of unsafe improvised behaviour. Environmental conditions such as night-time operation, deck slope, and vessel movement while manoeuvring compound these vulnerabilities. Critically, the absence of passenger-facing safety information, access controls to safety-critical spaces, and crew training in mobility aid risk management constitutes a systemic latent failure within the Safety Management System that permits these foreseeable incidents to occur repeatedly across diverse vessel types.

- **Occurrence c923c2a1-b237-f011-8c4d-6045bd0d3022**: An inland passenger vessels crew member was struck by a passenger mobility scooter, resulting in superficial injuries. Passenger lost control of there mobility scooter and accelerated across the wheel
- **Occurrence 73d30425-c19e-f011-bbd3-7c1e52038f97**: A passenger on board a paddle steamer was leaning down to tie their shoelace when they fell over and fractured their femur. The vessel was on its way to a berth and the local search and rescue vessel

### Passenger Falls Due to Vessel Motion and Night Conditions *(Exhibited in 31 incidents)*

This theme is characterised by passengers sustaining injuries — predominantly fractures and head trauma — as a result of losing balance aboard moving vessels, predominantly during night-time hours. The incidents share a consistent pattern: vessel motion in sea states ranging from calm to moderate is sufficient to destabilise passengers who are undertaking routine activities such as walking through cabins or standing from seated positions. The recurrence of these incidents suggests a systemic failure to treat vessel motion as a standing environmental hazard requiring active risk management, rather than an accepted background condition. The absence of passenger briefings on safe movement techniques, the inconsistent application of movement restrictions during adverse conditions, and the lack of verified handrail and non-slip infrastructure at incident locations collectively indicate that passenger safety culture aboard these vessels is insufficiently embedded at an operational level.

From a latent causation perspective, the hardware environment — specifically the provision, visibility, and accessibility of handrails, grab rails, and non-slip surfaces — remains unverified across multiple incidents, representing a potential design or maintenance deficiency that is never systematically audited following passenger falls. Human factors are equally significant: passengers are not reliably equipped with awareness of how to brace, grip, or anticipate vessel movement, and crew do not appear to consistently reinforce safe movement behaviours. Environmental conditions, particularly the compounding effect of night-time lighting on spatial awareness and balance correction response, are underweighted in onboard risk assessments. The discrepancy between measured wave heights and reported sea states in several incidents further suggests that environmental monitoring and reporting practices may be insufficiently precise to support meaningful safety decision-making. Together, these latent gaps create conditions in which even routine vessel motion poses a foreseeable and preventable injury risk to passengers.

- **Occurrence fb8b187c-2acc-ef11-b8e9-00224800e42a**: A cruise ship passenger was seated on a couch in their cabin, they went to stand up and fell forward onto the left side hitting their upper arm on the floor. The passenger didn't remember how the inci
- **Occurrence 7156affa-d703-f011-bae3-0022481a4e86**: A cruise ships passenger fell with the ships movement resulting in closed fracture to the shoulder. Passenger lost there balance due to the ship's movement and fell forward, landing on there right sho
